FILM SUPPLEMENT

INTERNATIONAL NEWS

HOLLYWOOD AND THE US PRESIDENTIAL ELECTION

— Jieke

Hollywood has always seemed like an otherworld detached from politics. Campaigning politicians never set foot near the studio gates, and all the stars and actors seemed deprived of their right to vote. However, this year is different. Hundreds of stars will be voting in the US presidential election this year, a first for them in their lives.

The number of voters in Hollywood is also not small. After many campaigning politicians began to approach them, Hollywood became an area of political importance.

RKO Pictures held a grand banquet at its studio to entertain US Senate candidate McAdoo and formally introduced him to all its employees. Warner Brothers also held a political dance, with McAdoo as the special guest, inviting key personnel from various studios to participate. They also formed a Roosevelt-Garner Club, preparing to host a Hollywood civic assembly. Key Hollywood executives, such as Schenck, Lasky, Lehrman, Sidman, Cohn, Harry Cohn, and McCreary, have all expressed their support. There is a growing trend in the American film industry to fully support Roosevelt.

The situation at MGM is slightly different. Hearst is a passionate Democrat, but manager Mayer is a good friend of President Hoover and has stayed at the White House. Whether MGM personnel will support the Democrats or the Republicans is still uncertain.

Because of the economic panic and other personal interests, American film stars, both male and female, are united in voting this year. Deng Ailing, Meng Gaomei, Little Van Dyke, and Bowers were previously stage performers without a fixed residence, and this autumn marks their first time voting.

Noma Shearer grew up in Canada and recently became an American citizen. She hired a private tutor to help her study political economy and American history. This year, she is also voting for the first time. Mary Pickford is also a Canadian native. She became an American citizen a few years ago when she married Owen Moore. She and Van Dyke are among the more politically minded stars, so they are unwilling to give up their right to vote whenever they have the opportunity. They are especially concerned this year. Van Dyke is currently traveling abroad and has asked a representative to vote for him.

Marlene Dietrich, Charlie Chaplin, Eliza Landi, Clive Brook, and Silver Lai have not yet become American citizens, so they cannot vote this year. McDonald has always automatically given up his right to vote, but this year he has found the courage to vote.

Stars who have lived in Hollywood for a long time were previously uninterested in politics, so many have given up their right to vote. Corinne Griffith is one of them. Dayle, Hamilton, Gary Cooper, Binglio Haubipi, and Daniel are also this type of star who are unwilling to vote. Bi Bi has now given birth to Ning Xin'er. To fulfill her duty as a mother, she will vote this year. Constance Bennett has now become a wealthy woman, and she is voting this year because of tax issues.

There is also a Hewro Ji, who received sixty-six votes when the Democratic National Convention nominated a candidate for president.

Film Reviews

REVIEW OF "THE HUDDLE"

— Yannan

Since Valentino's death, the most revered male beauty in the film industry has always been Ramon Novarro, especially as he is popular with women all over the world.

He has made many talkies. Relying on his God-given golden voice, he is even more brilliant than in the silent era. The brilliance of his silent masterpieces, Ben-Hur and The Student Prince, has been overshadowed by the talkies Pagan Love Song, Call of All Flesh, and the recent Mata Hari.

The Huddle, which premiered at the Cathay today, fully demonstrates that his youth is still surging, and his art is moving into its golden age. In this film, he still knows how to use his talents to win the audience's sympathy, using his rural state and weakness in the first half to contrast with his bravery and liveliness in the second half, revealing the scene of an American hero. Although his singing is not much, it is enough for us to savor.

The supporting cast is not bad – of course, not as organized as in Mata Hari – but Maggie Evans is commendable. Henry Armetta, who plays the father, also interjects well, turning the film into a comedy throughout.

In terms of directing, no loopholes can be found. The photography and lighting can also be said to be commendable. The scenery seems to have been filmed at Yale University. The film pays close attention to the ball game scenes, the movements are fast and clear, and it must be admitted that this is the skill of the American film industry.

The film depicts a student, from the countryside to the urban university, reforming his actions and environment step by step, until graduation, using very short shots to capture the drama.

"A HAPPY YEAR"

— Xin

When we have some worries, we hope to forget them. Therefore, Warner Brothers and First National, etc., all agree that the 1933 films should focus on comedy, making the coming year "A Happy Year." Almost all the productions end with reunions, and stories with a sad taste are changed.

But with depression in our hearts and nowhere to vent, keeping it suppressed for a year is too much. What is to be done?

The four years in this film do not seem rough, and the dialogue is concise and clear. Every sentence is indispensable, so it is indeed very powerful.

The depiction of the lives of general "privileged children" at American asset universities is very realistic and interesting. Many people sing marching songs, which can also stir up people's excitement.

In short, this film is a college student romantic comedy. Although there is no deep meaning beyond that, it is at least worth appreciating.

Film Studio Anecdotes

THE HARDSHIPS OF EXTRAS

— Mai

Moonlight Pictures. Since taking over the Great Wall Studio, the films they have produced have all been sold to the South Sea Islands overseas. Therefore, martial arts dramas are their main output.

Yesterday, they started shooting the "Fighting Arena" scene. The extras in the scene numbered forty-five people, and two cameras worked simultaneously. One took close-ups, and the other took wide shots. In theory, it should be faster than shooting with a single camera. However, they prepared from 3:00 PM until 4:00 AM the next morning before they finished. Therefore, the extras suffered greatly. The men had nothing to say, but the women were pitiful. Their families only knew that they could go back at 7:00 or 8:00 PM. They couldn't leave until their work was done. The anxiety in their hearts and the cold wind after the rain tormented them. They couldn't help but cry with resentment. It was so late, and they couldn't call a car. The company's gate was locked like a prison. They truly felt like they couldn't fly away even with wings. The thinner their clothes, the more their limbs trembled. Their stomachs were also empty. They were truly suffering from hunger and cold. The male extras took off their coats and put them on the women. But their stomachs were still unfed. They suffered for a night and half a day. The price they paid was only $1.20 (for women) and $1.00 (for men).

THE BEAUTIFUL DANCES OF THE ANN SUMMERS STUDIOS DANCE SCHOOL

Ann Summers Studios Dance School, founded in 1927, is the leading dance school in Shanghai. It holds a beautiful dance (Nick Nax) performance once a year. This year, it is scheduled for November 9th, 10th, and 11th at the Summer Peacock Theater on Jing'an Temple Road. The image on the left is the dance posture of Ms. Xie Meizhen of the school.

FILM TREATISE

THE AMERICAN METHOD OF MANUFACTURING STARS

— Chen Dabei

The term "star" was originally borrowed by people in the arts in Europe and the United States to describe an outstanding actor, speaker, or socialite. In Western theaters, calling someone a "star" is like calling them a "pillar" or "major performer" in Chinese theaters. Have you ever seen a large five-pointed star drawn on the door of a backstage dressing room in an American film? The words "Star Room" are written on the door of this dressing room. Without outstanding artistic cultivation, one cannot naturally enter this star room to be the protagonist.

Film is the most developed in the United States. The psychology of worshiping stars naturally pushes Americans to be number one. Because they want to attract public attention to these stars, they do not hesitate to sacrifice a large amount of advertising fees. They deliberately instruct these stars to create a little commotion in social circles and all lively occasions, so that these newspapers that receive huge advertising fees can publish the news in special large print, indirectly allowing the theaters showing their films to be crowded with people every performance.

Originally, the psychology of the masses, like that of civilized and barbaric societies, is to worship great heroes and great men. However, in American society, which lacks internal turmoil and external troubles, how can one easily establish extraordinary achievements or create great undertakings? Americans use this defect, using thousands of candles' worth of automatic electric lights to dazzle the large but empty star signboard, shifting the masses' psychology of worshiping heroes to worshiping stars. However, there are too many film companies in the United States. How can the stage and schools produce so many stars? Therefore, the jokes about manufacturing stars emerge one after another. Mary Pickford became famous, so village girls whose appearance and posture were slightly similar to Mary were invited to the city by film company representatives who came from thousands of miles away and were forced to play the lead role in a film.

European star and black-haired actress Lilian Harvey in Congress Dances.

READER'S SPRING AND AUTUMN

TALKING ABOUT "SUNSHINE SUSIE"

In the past, British films generally gave us a bad impression. Their aristocratic expressions and many meaningless dialogues were truly boring. Who would have thought that this film, Sunshine Susie, would be a big exception?

When I walked into the Carlton Theater, knowing that it was a British film, I felt a inexplicable sense of loss in my heart. But when I walked out of that door after the show, I felt an unsurpassed pleasure and satisfaction.

The plot is very ordinary, a purely American-style love story. But the director was able to perform it with extreme enthusiasm, as if giving the audience a cup of rich coffee.

The actors are rarely seen by us, but their wonderful performances can be said to be "a blockbuster." Renate Muller and Jack Holbert are light and delicate, and can be chased after by Irene Dunne and McDonald. Everything in this film follows the same path as several famous "Scandal" films.

The appearance of each actor is under extremely natural conditions, avoiding the common European film problem of being far-fetched. Whenever there are some calmer scenes, appropriate interpolations are used, which are humorous and touching. The whole film consistently uses low-pitched music, and whenever there is a small opportunity, songs are used to represent dialogue. What is not slightly let go of is the sound of trains typing and other machines, which are also matched into a beautiful rhythm.

Even so, several actors have not detached themselves from the flavor of European continental opera. Of course, this flavor is extremely exciting. If we talk about the film in general, it is indeed similar to "Monte Carlo" in some ways, and it has all the scenery, performance, rhythm, and songs of "Monte Carlo," only the plot is different.

Irrespective of Age

— Ai

In Hollywood, age is not a concern. Marie Dressler is already sixty years old. But in 1932, she was elected queen! The reputation and welcome she enjoyed surpassed any young woman's wildest dreams. John Barrymore is fifty-one years old this year. Not only is he still regarded as a major star, but he also often plays young lovers. He is even more popular than younger stars. There is also a Chio Sale, whose face is beautiful and who is not yet thirty years old. Yet her success lies in her portrayal of white-haired old women. Dorothy Petrems is only in her prime, but she permanently plays older mothers. Few people can notice her youthful beauty.

Submission Guidelines for the Shen Bao Film Supplement

The supplement welcomes submissions and offers generous compensation upon publication.

Once a piece is published, the copyright belongs to the Shen Bao.

If the submission is a translation, the original work should be included.

Due to limited space, articles should be concise, ideally around 500 words.

Manuscripts, photographs, etc., will not be returned unless a self-addressed stamped envelope is provided.

Submissions that are sent to multiple publications, plagiarized, or copied from instruction manuals will not be compensated.

Submissions must include a seal; otherwise, they will not be compensated.

Do not write on both sides of the manuscript paper.

Overseas News

Income Tax of Hollywood Stars

— Mei Cun

The income tax for Hollywood stars has been revised this year with a new tax rate, roughly more than double what it was last year. Consequently, most stars feel uneasy, especially foreign stars, who, in addition to paying taxes in Hollywood, also have to bear the tax burden of their home countries. For example, Charlie Chaplin has to pay income tax to his home country (England), which amounts to 25% of his total annual income. Maurice Chevalier also has to pay a similar amount to France.

Big star John Gilbert makes two films a year, earning US$500,000. He also has other considerable income. Last year, he paid 20% of his total income in taxes. However, this year he has to pay US$263,710 (52.7% of his total salary)—how significant!

Will Rogers' salary is US$450,000 per year, and he also has a considerable additional income. Although we cannot accurately estimate his total annual income, his income tax certainly accounts for more than 50% of his income. Charlie Chaplin's film "City Lights" has already grossed more than US$3 million. Calculated at an income tax rate of 57.12%, this number is quite staggering.

Ann Harding made four films this year, with a fee of US$80,000 per film. Her income tax this year, calculated at 46%, requires her to pay more than US$147,000 in taxes. In next year's contract, she can earn a fee of US$100,000 per film, so her income tax will also increase to more than US$180,000.

Ruth Chatterton and William Powell signed a two-year contract with Warner Bros. They will make three films in the first year at US$100,000 per film, and three films in the second year, increasing to US$125,000 per film. Therefore, their income tax next year will be more than US$170,000 each.

Tallulah Bankhead, Maurice Chevalier, Wallace Beery, Ramon Novarro, Buster Keaton, and others have salaries between US$200,000 and US$300,000. The income tax they have to pay is approximately between 40% and 45%, which is also around US$100,000.
